     <p>
      <b>
       Isoroku Yamamoto
      </b>
      <span style="font-weight: normal">
       (
       <span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">
         
       </span>
       <span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">
        ,
       </span>
       <i>
        Yamamoto Isoroku
       </i>
       <sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets">
         <span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">
          ?
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       , April 4, 1884 April 18, 1943)
      </span>
      was a
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">
       Japanese
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshal_(Imperial_Japanese_Navy)" title="Marshal (Imperial Japanese Navy)">
       Marshal Admiral
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">
       commander-in-chief
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Combined_Fleet" title="Combined Fleet">
       Combined Fleet
      </a>
      during World War II until his death.
     </p>
     <p>
      Yamamoto held several important posts in the Imperial Japanese Navy, and undertook many of its changes and reorganizations, especially its development of naval aviation. He was the commander-in-chief during the decisive early years of the
      <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">
       Pacific War
      </a>
      and so was responsible for major battles such as
      <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">
       Pearl Harbor
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">
       Midway
      </a>
      . He died when American codebreakers
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Vengeance" title="Operation Vengeance">
       identified his flight plans and his plane was shot down
      </a>
      . His death was a major blow to Japanese military morale during World War II.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16">
        <a href="#Sources">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          13
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          Sources
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17">
        <a href="#External_links">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          14
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          External links
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background">
       Family background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Family background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="204" data-file-width="262" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          does not
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
           cite
          </a>
          any
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           references or sources
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help improve this section by
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">
           adding citations to reliable sources
          </a>
          . Unsourced material may be challenged and
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           removed
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (May 2010)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Yamamoto was born
      <b>
       Isoroku Takano
      </b>
      <span style="font-weight: normal">
       (
       <span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">
         
       </span>
       <span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">
        ,
       </span>
       <i>
        Takano Isoroku
       </i>
       <sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets">
         <span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">
          ?
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       )
      </span>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Nagaoka,_Niigata" title="Nagaoka, Niigata">
       Nagaoka
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Niigata_Prefecture" title="Niigata Prefecture">
       Niigata
      </a>
      . His father was Sadayoshi Takano ( ), an intermediate-rank
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">
        samurai
       </a>
      </i>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Nagaoka_Domain" title="Nagaoka Domain">
       Nagaoka Domain
      </a>
      . "Isoroku" is an old Japanese term meaning "56"; the name referred to his father's age at Isoroku's birth.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1916, Isoroku was adopted into the
      <a href="/wiki/Yamamoto_Tatewaki" title="Yamamoto Tatewaki">
       Yamamoto family
      </a>
      (another family of former Nagaoka samurai) and took the Yamamoto name. It was a common practice for samurai families lacking sons to adopt suitable young men in this fashion to carry on the family name, the rank and the income that comes with it. In 1918 Isoroku married Reiko Mihashi, with whom he had two sons and two daughters.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">
       Early career
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early career">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      After graduating from the
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Naval_Academy" title="Imperial Japanese Naval Academy">
       Imperial Japanese Naval Academy
      </a>
      in 1904, Yamamoto served on the
      <a href="/wiki/Armored_cruiser" title="Armored cruiser">
       armored cruiser
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cruiser_Nisshin" title="Japanese cruiser Nisshin">
       <i>
        Nisshin
       </i>
      </a>
      during the
      <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">
       Russo-Japanese War
      </a>
      . He was wounded at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tsushima" title="Battle of Tsushima">
       Battle of Tsushima
      </a>
      , losing two fingers (the index and middle fingers) on his left hand, as the cruiser was hit repeatedly by the Russian battleline. He returned to the
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_War_College_(Japan)" title="Naval War College (Japan)">
       Naval Staff College
      </a>
      in 1914, emerging as a
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_ranks_of_the_Japanese_Empire_during_World_War_II" title="Naval ranks of the Japanese Empire during World War II">
       Lieutenant Commander
      </a>
      in 1916.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1920s_and_1930s">
       1920s and 1930s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1920s and 1930s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Yamamoto was part of the Japanese Navy establishment, who were rivals of the more aggressive Army establishment, especially the officers of the
      <a href="/wiki/Kwantung_Army" title="Kwantung Army">
       Kwantung Army
      </a>
      . As such he promoted a policy of a strong fleet to project force through
      <a href="/wiki/Gunboat_diplomacy" title="Gunboat diplomacy">
       gunboat diplomacy
      </a>
      , rather than a fleet used primarily for transport of invasion land forces, as some of his political opponents in the army wanted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This stance led him to oppose the invasion of China. He also opposed war against the United States partly because of his studies at
      <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">
       Harvard University
      </a>
      (19191921) and his two postings as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_attach%C3%A9" title="Naval attach">
       naval attach
      </a>
      in Washington, D.C. He learned to speak fluent English as a result. Yamamoto traveled extensively in the United States during his tour of duty there, where he studied American customs and business practices.
     </p>
     <p>
      He was promoted to
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_ranks_of_the_Japanese_Empire_during_World_War_II" title="Naval ranks of the Japanese Empire during World War II">
       Captain
      </a>
      in 1923. On April 13, 1924 at the rank of captain, he was part of the Japanese delegation visiting the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Naval_War_College" title="U.S. Naval War College">
       U.S. Naval War College
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later that year, he changed his specialty from
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_artillery" title="Naval artillery">
       gunnery
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_aviation" title="Naval aviation">
       naval aviation
      </a>
      . His first command was the cruiser
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cruiser_Isuzu" title="Japanese cruiser Isuzu">
       <i>
        Isuzu
       </i>
      </a>
      in 1928, followed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">
       aircraft carrier
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Akagi" title="Japanese aircraft carrier Akagi">
       <i>
        Akagi
       </i>
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="740" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg/220px-H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg/330px-H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg/440px-H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H78628_Isoroku_Yamamoto.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Isoroku Yamamoto with
        <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">
         United States Secretary of the Navy
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Curtis_D._Wilbur" title="Curtis D. Wilbur">
         Curtis D. Wilbur
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      He participated in the second
      <a href="/wiki/London_Naval_Conference" title="London Naval Conference">
       London Naval Conference
      </a>
      of 1930 as a
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_ranks_of_the_Japanese_Empire_during_World_War_II" title="Naval ranks of the Japanese Empire during World War II">
       Rear Admiral
      </a>
      and the 1934 London Naval Conference as a
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_ranks_of_the_Japanese_Empire_during_World_War_II" title="Naval ranks of the Japanese Empire during World War II">
       Vice Admiral
      </a>
      , as the growing military influence on the government at the time deemed that a career military specialist needed to accompany the diplomats to the arms limitations talks. Yamamoto was a strong proponent of
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_aviation" title="Naval aviation">
       naval aviation
      </a>
      , and served as head of the Aeronautics Department before accepting a post as commander of the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Carrier_Division" title="First Carrier Division">
       First Carrier Division
      </a>
      . Yamamoto personally opposed the invasion of
      <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">
       Manchuria
      </a>
      in 1931, the subsequent
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">
       land war with China (1937)
      </a>
      , and the 1940
      <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">
       Tripartite Pact
      </a>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
       Nazi Germany
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (18611946)">
       Fascist Italy
      </a>
      . As Deputy Navy Minister, he apologized to United States Ambassador
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_C._Grew" title="Joseph C. Grew">
       Joseph C. Grew
      </a>
      for the bombing of the
      <a href="/wiki/Gunboat" title="Gunboat">
       gunboat
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/USS_Panay_(PR-5)" title="USS Panay (PR-5)">
       USS
       <i>
        Panay
       </i>
      </a>
      in December 1937. These issues made him a target of assassination threats by pro-war militarists.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japan-navy-1931-1944-sleeve_30-1-.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="199" data-file-width="110" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Japan-navy-1931-1944-sleeve_30-1-.gif" width="110"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japan-navy-1931-1944-sleeve_30-1-.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Sleeve insignia of
        <a href="/wiki/Naval_ranks_of_the_Japanese_Empire_during_World_War_II" title="Naval ranks of the Japanese Empire during World War II">
         Kaigun Taish (Admiral)
        </a>
        ; the rank Yamamoto held at the time of his death.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Throughout 1938, many young army and naval officers began to speak publicly against Yamamoto and certain other Japanese admirals such as
      <a href="/wiki/Mitsumasa_Yonai" title="Mitsumasa Yonai">
       Mitsumasa Yonai
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Shigeyoshi_Inoue" title="Shigeyoshi Inoue">
       Shigeyoshi Inoue
      </a>
      for their strong opposition towards a Tripartite pact with Nazi Germany for reportedly being against "Japan's natural interests."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Yamamoto himself received a steady stream of hate mail and death threats from Japanese nationalists but his reaction to the prospect of death by assassination was passive and accepting. The Admiral wrote:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       To die for Emperor and Nation is the highest hope of a military man. After a brave hard fight the blossoms are scattered on the fighting field. But if a person wants to take a life instead, still the fighting man will go to eternity for Emperor and country. One man's life or death is a matter of no importance. All that matters is the Empire. As
       <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">
        Confucius
       </a>
       said, "They may crush cinnabar, yet they do not take away its color; one may burn a fragrant herb, yet it will not destroy the scent." They may destroy my body, yet they will not take away my will.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         6
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The Japanese army, annoyed at Yamamoto's unflinching opposition to a Rome-Berlin-Tokyo treaty, dispatched military police to "guard" Yamamoto; this was an attempt by the Army to keep an eye on him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He was later reassigned from the
      <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Navy_of_Japan" title="Ministry of the Navy of Japan">
       Navy Ministry
      </a>
      to sea as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commander-in-Chief" title="Commander-in-Chief">
       Commander-in-Chief
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Combined_Fleet" title="Japanese Combined Fleet">
       Combined Fleet
      </a>
      on (August 30, 1939). This was done as one of the last acts of the then-acting Navy Minister
      <a href="/wiki/Mitsumasa_Yonai" title="Mitsumasa Yonai">
       Mitsumasa Yonai
      </a>
      , under
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baron_Hiranuma" title="Baron Hiranuma">
       Baron Hiranuma
      </a>
      's short-lived administration partly to make it harder for assassins to target Yamamoto; Yonai was certain that if Yamamoto remained ashore, he would be killed before the year (1939) ended.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1940.E2.80.931941">
       19401941
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 19401941">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Yamamoto was promoted to
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_ranks_of_the_Japanese_Empire_during_World_War_II" title="Naval ranks of the Japanese Empire during World War II">
       Admiral
      </a>
      on November 15, 1940. This, in spite of the fact that when
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hideki_T%C5%8Dj%C5%8D" title="Hideki Tj">
       Hideki Tj
      </a>
      was appointed Prime Minister on October 18, 1941, many political observers thought that Yamamoto's career was essentially over.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt114_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoyt114-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tj had been Yamamoto's old opponent from the time when the latter served as Japan's deputy navy minister and Tj was the prime mover behind Japan's takeover of
      <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">
       Manchuria
      </a>
      . It was believed that Yamamoto would be appointed to command the Yokosuka Naval Base, "a nice safe demotion with a big house and no power at all."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt114_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoyt114-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the new Japanese cabinet was announced, however, Yamamoto found himself left alone in his position despite his open conflicts with Tj and other members of the army's oligarchy who favored war with the European powers and America. Two of the main reasons for Yamamoto's political survival were his immense popularity within the fleet, where he commanded the respect of his men and officers, and his close relations with the imperial family.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He also had the acceptance by Japan's naval hierarchy:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "There was no officer more competent to lead the Combined Fleet to victory than Admiral Yamamoto. His daring plan for the Pearl Harbor attack had passed through the crucible of the Japanese naval establishment, and after many expressed misgivings, his fellow admirals had realized that Yamamoto spoke no more than the truth when he said that Japan's hope for victory in this [upcoming] war was limited by time and oil. Every sensible officer of the navy was well aware of the perennial oil problems. Also, it had to be recognized that if the enemy could seriously disturb Japanese merchant shipping, then the fleet would be endangered even more."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         11
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Consequently, Yamamoto stayed in his post. With Tj now in charge of Japan's highest political office, it became clear the army would lead the navy into a war about which Yamamoto had serious reservations. He wrote to an ultranationalist:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Should hostilities once break out between Japan and the United States, it would not be enough that we take
       <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
        Guam
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       , nor even Hawaii and San Francisco. To make victory certain, we would have to march into
       <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">
        Washington
       </a>
       and dictate the terms of peace in the
       <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">
        White House
       </a>
       . I wonder if our politicians (who speak so lightly of a Japanese-American war) have confidence as to the final outcome and are prepared to make the necessary sacrifices.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PrangeAt_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PrangeAt-12">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         12
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      This quote was spread by the militarists, minus the last sentence, where it was interpreted in America as a boast that Japan would conquer the entire continental United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PrangeAt_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PrangeAt-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The omitted sentence showed Yamamoto's counsel of caution towards a war that could cost Japan dearly. Nevertheless, Yamamoto accepted the reality of impending war and planned for a quick victory by destroying the US fleet at Pearl Harbor in a
      <a href="/wiki/Preventive_war" title="Preventive war">
       preventive strike
      </a>
      while simultaneously thrusting into the oil and rubber resource rich areas of Southeast Asia, especially the Dutch East Indies, Borneo and Malaya. In naval matters, Yamamoto opposed the building of the super-
      <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">
       battleships
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_battleship_Yamato" title="Japanese battleship Yamato">
       <i>
        Yamato
       </i>
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_battleship_Musashi" title="Japanese battleship Musashi">
       <i>
        Musashi
       </i>
      </a>
      as an unwise investment of resources.
     </p>
     <p>
      Yamamoto was responsible for a number of innovations in
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy_Air_Service" title="Imperial Japanese Navy Air Service">
       Japanese naval aviation
      </a>
      . Although remembered for his association with aircraft carriers due to Pearl Harbor and Midway, Yamamoto did more to influence the development of land-based naval aviation, particularly the
      <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_G3M" title="Mitsubishi G3M">
       Mitsubishi G3M
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_G4M" title="Mitsubishi G4M">
       G4M
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Medium_bomber" title="Medium bomber">
       medium bombers
      </a>
      . His demand for great range and the ability to carry a
      <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">
       torpedo
      </a>
      was intended to conform to Japanese conceptions of attriting the American fleet as it advanced across the Pacific in war. The planes did achieve long range, but long-range fighter escorts were not available. These planes were lightly constructed and when fully fueled, they were especially vulnerable to enemy fire. This earned the G4M the sardonic nickname "the Flying Cigarette Lighter." Yamamoto would eventually die in one of these aircraft.
     </p>
     <p>
      The range of the G3M and G4M contributed to a demand for great range in a fighter aircraft. This partly drove the requirements for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A6M_Zero" title="A6M Zero">
       A6M Zero
      </a>
      which was as noteworthy for its range as for its maneuverability. Both qualities were again purchased at the expense of light construction and flammability that later contributed to the A6M's high casualty rates as the war progressed.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamamoto_h63430.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="733" data-file-width="595" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Yamamoto_h63430.jpg/220px-Yamamoto_h63430.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Yamamoto_h63430.jpg/330px-Yamamoto_h63430.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Yamamoto_h63430.jpg/440px-Yamamoto_h63430.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yamamoto_h63430.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Yamamoto, U.S. file photo
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As Japan moved toward war during 1940, Yamamoto gradually moved toward strategic as well as tactical innovation, again with mixed results. Prompted by talented young officers such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Commander" title="Lieutenant Commander">
       Lieutenant Commander
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Minoru_Genda" title="Minoru Genda">
       Minoru Genda
      </a>
      , Yamamoto approved the reorganization of Japanese carrier forces into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Air_Fleet" title="First Air Fleet">
       First Air Fleet
      </a>
      , a consolidated striking force that gathered Japan's six largest carriers into one unit. This innovation gave great striking capacity, but also concentrated the vulnerable carriers into a compact target; both boon and bane would be realized in war. Yamamoto also oversaw the organization of a similar large land-based organization in the 11th Air Fleet, which would later use the G3M and G4M to neutralize American air forces in the
      <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
       Philippines
      </a>
      and sink the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
       British
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Force_Z" title="Force Z">
       Force "Z"
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In January 1941, Yamamoto went even further and proposed a radical revision of Japanese naval strategy. For two decades, in keeping with the
      <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">
       doctrine
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_T._Mahan" title="Alfred T. Mahan">
       Captain Alfred T. Mahan
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy_General_Staff" title="Imperial Japanese Navy General Staff">
       Naval General Staff
      </a>
      had planned in terms of Japanese light surface forces,
      <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">
       submarines
      </a>
      and land-based air units whittling down the American Fleet as it advanced across the Pacific until the
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">
       Japanese Navy
      </a>
      engaged it in a climactic "Decisive Battle" in the northern
      <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea" title="Philippine Sea">
       Philippine Sea
      </a>
      (between the
      <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">
       Ryukyu Islands
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marianas_Islands" title="Marianas Islands">
       Marianas Islands
      </a>
      ), with
      <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">
       battleships
      </a>
      meeting in the traditional exchange between
      <a href="/wiki/Line_of_battle" title="Line of battle">
       battle lines
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Correctly pointing out this plan had never worked even in Japanese war games, and painfully aware of American strategic advantages in military productive capacity, Yamamoto proposed instead to seek a decision with the Americans by first reducing their forces with a preventive strike, and following it with a "Decisive Battle" fought offensively, rather than defensively. Yamamoto hoped, but probably did not believe, if the Americans could be dealt such terrific blows early in the war, they might be willing to negotiate an end to the conflict. As it turned out, however, the note officially breaking diplomatic relations with the United States was delivered late, and he correctly perceived the Americans would be resolved upon revenge and unwilling to negotiate. At the end of the attack upon Pearl Harbor, upon hearing of the mis-timing of the communique breaking diplomatic relations with the United States earlier that day,
      <a href="/wiki/Isoroku_Yamamoto%27s_sleeping_giant_quote" title="Isoroku Yamamoto's sleeping giant quote">
       it is reputed Yamamoto said
      </a>
      , "I fear all we have done today is to awaken a great, sleeping giant and fill him with a terrible resolve.";
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      however, there is no documented evidence the statement was made.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Naval General Staff proved reluctant to go along and Yamamoto was eventually driven to capitalize on his popularity in the fleet by threatening to resign to get his way. Admiral
      <a href="/wiki/Osami_Nagano" title="Osami Nagano">
       Osami Nagano
      </a>
      and the Naval General Staff eventually caved in to this pressure, but only insofar as approving the
      <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">
       attack on Pearl Harbor
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The First Air Fleet commenced preparations for the Pearl Harbor Raid, solving a number of technical problems along the way, including how to launch torpedoes in the shallow water of Pearl Harbor and how to craft armor-piercing bombs by machining down battleship gun projectiles.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor">
       Attack on Pearl Harbor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Attack on Pearl Harbor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Events_leading_to_the_attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Events leading to the attack on Pearl Harbor">
       Events leading to the attack on Pearl Harbor
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">
       Attack on Pearl Harbor
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      As Yamamoto had planned, the First Air Fleet of six carriers commenced hostilities against the Americans on December 7, 1941, launching 353
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parillo288_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-parillo288-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      aircraft against Pearl Harbor in two waves. The attack was a complete success according to the parameters of the mission which sought to sink at least four American battleships and prevent the U.S. Fleet from interfering in Japan's southward advance for at least six months. American aircraft carriers were also considered a choice target, but these were not in port at the time of the attack.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="499" data-file-width="370" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg/220px-Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg/330px-Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yamamoto-Isoroku.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Isoroku Yamamoto
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the end, five American battleships were sunk, three were damaged, and eleven other
      <a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">
       cruisers
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">
       destroyers
      </a>
      and auxiliaries were sunk or seriously damaged. The Japanese lost only 29 aircraft, while 74 were damaged from anti-aircraft fire from the ground. The damaged aircraft were disproportionately
      <a href="/wiki/Dive_bomber" title="Dive bomber">
       dive
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_bomber" title="Torpedo bomber">
       torpedo bombers
      </a>
      , seriously impacting available firepower to exploit the first two waves' success, so the commander of the First Air Fleet, Naval Lieutenant-General
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chuichi_Nagumo" title="Chuichi Nagumo">
       Chuichi Nagumo
      </a>
      , withdrew. Yamamoto later lamented Nagumo's failure to seize the initiative to seek out and destroy the American carriers, absent from the harbor, or further bombard various strategically important facilities on
      <a href="/wiki/Oahu" title="Oahu">
       Oahu
      </a>
      . Nagumo had absolutely no idea where the American carriers might be, and remaining on station while his forces cast about looking for them ran the risk of his own forces being found first and attacked while his aircraft were absent searching. In any case, insufficient daylight remained after recovering the aircraft from the first two waves for the carriers to launch and recover a third before dark, and Nagumo's escorting destroyers lacked the fuel capacity for him to loiter long. Much has been made of Yamamoto's hindsight, but (in keeping with Japanese military tradition not to criticize the commander on the spot),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      he did not punish Nagumo in any way for his withdrawal.
     </p>
     <p>
      On the strategic level, the attack was a disaster for Japan, rousing American passions for revenge due to it being a "sneak attack". The shock of the attack coming in an unexpected place, with such devastating results and without the expected "fair play" of a
      <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war" title="Declaration of war">
       declaration of war
      </a>
      galvanized the American public's determination to avenge the attack. When asked by Prime Minister
      <a href="/wiki/Fumimaro_Konoe" title="Fumimaro Konoe">
       Fumimaro Konoe
      </a>
      in mid-1941 concerning the outcome of a possible war with the United States, Yamamoto made a well-known and prophetic statement: If ordered to fight, "I shall run wild considerably for the first six months or a year, but I have utterly no confidence for the second and third years."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His prediction would be vindicated as Japan easily conquered territories and islands for the first six months of the war until it suffered a shattering defeat at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">
       Battle of Midway
      </a>
      on June 47, 1942, which ultimately tilted the balance of power in the Pacific towards the U.S.
     </p>
     <p>
      As a strategic blow intended to prevent American interference in the
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">
       Dutch East Indies
      </a>
      for six months, the Pearl Harbor attack was a success, but unbeknownst to Yamamoto, it was a pointless one. The U.S. Navy had abandoned any intention of attempting to charge across the Pacific towards the Philippines at the outset of war in 1935 (in keeping with the evolution of
      <a href="/wiki/War_Plan_Orange" title="War Plan Orange">
       War Plan Orange
      </a>
      ). In 1937, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
       U.S. Navy
      </a>
      had further determined even fully manning the fleet to wartime levels could not be accomplished in less than six months, and myriad other logistic assets needed to execute a trans-Pacific movement simply did not exist and would require two years to construct after the onset of war. In 1940, U.S.
      <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Operations" title="Chief of Naval Operations">
       Chief of Naval Operations
      </a>
      , Admiral
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_Rainsford_Stark" title="Harold Rainsford Stark">
       Harold Stark
      </a>
      had penned a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plan_Dog_memorandum" title="Plan Dog memorandum">
       Plan Dog memorandum
      </a>
      , which emphasized a defensive war in the Pacific while the U.S. concentrated on defeating
      <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
       Nazi Germany
      </a>
      first, and consigned Admiral
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Husband_Kimmel" title="Husband Kimmel">
       Husband Kimmel
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Pacific_Fleet" title="United States Pacific Fleet">
       Pacific Fleet
      </a>
      to merely keeping the Imperial Japanese Navy (IJN) out of the eastern Pacific and away from the shipping lanes to Australia. Moreover, it is in question whether the U.S. would have gone to war at all had Japan only attacked British and Dutch possessions in the Far East.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="December_1941_to_May_1942">
       December 1941 to May 1942
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: December 1941 to May 1942">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      With the American Fleet largely neutralized at Pearl Harbor, Yamamoto's Combined Fleet turned to the task of executing the larger Japanese war plan devised by the
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army" title="Imperial Japanese Army">
       Imperial Japanese Army
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy_General_Staff" title="Imperial Japanese Navy General Staff">
       Navy General Staff
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Air_Fleet" title="First Air Fleet">
       First Air Fleet
      </a>
      proceeded to make a circuit of the Pacific, striking American, Australian, Dutch and British installations from
      <a href="/wiki/Wake_Island" title="Wake Island">
       Wake Island
      </a>
      to Australia to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">
       Ceylon
      </a>
      in the Indian Ocean. The 11th Air Fleet caught the American 5th Air Force on the ground in the Philippines hours after Pearl Harbor, and then proceeded to sink the British Force "Z" (battleship
      <a href="/wiki/HMS_Prince_of_Wales_(53)" title="HMS Prince of Wales (53)">
       HMS
       <i>
        Prince of Wales
       </i>
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Battlecruiser" title="Battlecruiser">
       battlecruiser
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/HMS_Repulse_(1916)" title="HMS Repulse (1916)">
       HMS
       <i>
        Repulse
       </i>
      </a>
      ) underway at sea.
     </p>
     <p>
      Under Yamamoto's able subordinates, Naval Lieutenant-Generals
      <a href="/wiki/Jisabur%C5%8D_Ozawa" title="Jisabur Ozawa">
       Jisabur Ozawa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nobutake_Kond%C5%8D" title="Nobutake Kond">
       Nobutake Kond
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ib%C5%8D_Takahashi" title="Ib Takahashi">
       Ib Takahashi
      </a>
      , the Japanese swept the inadequate remaining American, British, Dutch and Australian naval assets from the Dutch East Indies in a series of amphibious landings and surface naval battles that culminated in the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Java_Sea" title="Battle of the Java Sea">
       Battle of the Java Sea
      </a>
      on February 27, 1942. With the occupation of the Dutch East Indies, and the reduction of the remaining American positions in the
      <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
       Philippines
      </a>
      on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bataan_Peninsula" title="Bataan Peninsula">
       Bataan Peninsula
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Corregidor" title="Corregidor">
       Corregidor
      </a>
      island, the Japanese had secured their oil- and rubber-rich "Southern Resources Area".
     </p>
     <p>
      By late March, having achieved their initial aims with surprising speed and little loss (albeit against enemies ill-prepared to resist them), the Japanese paused to consider their next moves. Yamamoto and a few Japanese military leaders and officials waited, hoping that the United States or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">
       Great Britain
      </a>
      would negotiate for an armistice or a peace treaty to end the war in their favour. But when the British, as well as the Americans, expressed no interest in negotiating with Japan for any cease fire, the Japanese thoughts turned to securing their newly seized territory and acquiring more with an eye toward attempting to force one or more of their enemies out of the war.
     </p>
     <p>
      Competing plans were developed at this stage, including thrusts to the west against
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">
       India
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Proposed_Japanese_invasion_of_Australia_during_World_War_II" title="Proposed Japanese invasion of Australia during World War II">
       south against Australia
      </a>
      and the east against the United States. Yamamoto was involved in this debate, supporting different plans at different times with varying degrees of enthusiasm and for varying purposes, including
      <a href="/wiki/Vote_trading" title="Vote trading">
       "horse-trading"
      </a>
      for support of his own objectives.
     </p>
     <p>
      Plans included ideas as ambitious as invading India or Australia, or seizing Hawaii. These grandiose ventures were inevitably set aside as the army could not spare enough troops from China for the first two (which would require a minimum of 250,000 men), nor shipping to support the latter two. (Shipping was allocated separately to IJN &amp; IJA, and jealously guarded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parillo_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-parillo-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) Instead, the Imperial General Staff supported an army thrust into
      <a href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">
       Burma
      </a>
      in hopes of linking up with Indian Nationalists revolting against British rule, and attacks in
      <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">
       New Guinea
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">
       Solomon Islands
      </a>
      designed to imperil Australia's sea line of communication with the United States. Yamamoto agitated for an offensive decisive battle in the east to finish off the American fleet, but the more conservative Naval General Staff officers were unwilling to risk it.
     </p>
     <p>
      On April 18, in the midst of these debates, the
      <a href="/wiki/Doolittle_Raid" title="Doolittle Raid">
       Doolittle Raid
      </a>
      struck Tokyo and the surrounding areas, galvanizing the threat posed by the American aircraft carriers in the minds of staff officers, and giving Yamamoto an event he could exploit to get his way as further debate over military strategy came to a quick end. The Naval General Staff agreed to Yamamoto's Midway (MI) Operation, subsequent to the first phase of the operations against Australia's link with America, and concurrent with their own plan to seize positions in the
      <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">
       Aleutian Islands
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Yamamoto rushed planning for the Midway and Aleutians missions, while dispatching a force under Naval Major-General
      <a href="/wiki/Takeo_Takagi" title="Takeo Takagi">
       Takeo Takagi
      </a>
      , including the
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Carrier_Division" title="Fifth Carrier Division">
       Fifth Carrier Division
      </a>
      (the large, new carriers
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Sh%C5%8Dkaku" title="Japanese aircraft carrier Shkaku">
       <i>
        Shkaku
       </i>
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Zuikaku" title="Japanese aircraft carrier Zuikaku">
       <i>
        Zuikaku
       </i>
      </a>
      ), to support the effort to seize the islands of
      <a href="/wiki/Tulagi" title="Tulagi">
       Tulagi
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guadalcanal_(Pacific_Ocean_island)" title="Guadalcanal (Pacific Ocean island)">
       Guadalcanal
      </a>
      for seaplane and aeroplane bases, and the town of
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Moresby" title="Port Moresby">
       Port Moresby
      </a>
      on Papua New Guinea's south coast facing Australia.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Mo" title="Operation Mo">
       Port Moresby (MO) Operation
      </a>
      proved an unwelcome setback. Although Tulagi and Guadalcanal were taken, the Port Moresby invasion fleet was compelled to turn back when Takagi clashed with an American carrier task force in the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Coral_Sea" title="Battle of the Coral Sea">
       Battle of the Coral Sea
      </a>
      in early May. Although the Japanese sank the American carrier
      <a href="/wiki/USS_Lexington_(CV-2)" title="USS Lexington (CV-2)">
       USS
       <i>
        Lexington
       </i>
      </a>
      and damaged the
      <a href="/wiki/USS_Yorktown_(CV-5)" title="USS Yorktown (CV-5)">
       USS
       <i>
        Yorktown
       </i>
       (CV-5)
      </a>
      , the Americans damaged the carrier
      <i>
       Shkaku
      </i>
      so badly that she required dockyard repairs, and the Japanese lost the light carrier
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shoho" title="Japanese aircraft carrier Shoho">
       <i>
        Shoho
       </i>
      </a>
      . Just as importantly, Japanese operational mishaps and American fighters and anti-aircraft fire devastated the dive bomber and torpedo plane formations of both
      <i>
       Shkaku
      </i>
      's and
      <i>
       Zuikaku
      </i>
      's air groups. These losses sidelined
      <i>
       Zuikaku
      </i>
      while she awaited replacement aircraft and aircrews, and saw to tactical integration and training. These two ships would be sorely missed a month later at Midway.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Midway.2C_June_1942">
       Battle of Midway, June 1942
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Battle of Midway, June 1942">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">
       Battle of Midway
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Yamamoto's plan for Midway Island was an extension of his efforts to knock the U.S. Pacific Fleet out of action long enough for Japan to fortify her defensive perimeter in the Pacific island chains. Yamamoto felt it necessary to seek an early, offensive decisive battle.
     </p>
     <p>
      This plan was long believed to have been to draw American attentionand possibly carrier forcesnorth from Pearl Harbor by sending his Fifth Fleet (two light carriers, five cruisers, 13 destroyers, and four transports) against the Aleutians,
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dutch_Harbor" title="Battle of Dutch Harbor">
       raiding
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Harbor" title="Dutch Harbor">
       Dutch Harbor
      </a>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Unalaska_Island" title="Unalaska Island">
       Unalaska Island
      </a>
      and invading the more distant islands of
      <a href="/wiki/Kiska" title="Kiska">
       Kiska
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Attu_Island" title="Attu Island">
       Attu
      </a>
      . Recent scholarship
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      using Japanese language documents has revealed it was, rather, an unrelated venture of the Naval General Staff which Yamamoto agreed to conduct concurrently with the Midway operation, in exchange for the latter's approval.
     </p>
     <p>
      While Fifth Fleet attacked the Aleutians, First Mobile Force (4 carriers, 2 battleships, 3 cruisers, and 12 destroyers) would raid Midway and destroy its air force. Once this was neutralized, Second Fleet (1 light carrier, 2 battleships, 10 cruisers, 21 destroyers, and 11 transports) would land 5,000 troops to seize the
      <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">
       atoll
      </a>
      from the American
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">
       Marines
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The seizure of Midway was expected to draw the American carriers west into a trap where the First Mobile Force would engage and destroy them. Afterward, First Fleet (1 light carrier, 7 battleships, 3 cruisers and 13 destroyers), in conjunction with elements of Second Fleet, would mop up remaining American surface forces and complete the destruction of the Pacific Fleet.
     </p>
     <p>
      To guard against mischance, Yamamoto initiated two security measures. The first was an aerial reconnaissance mission (Operation K) over Pearl Harbor to ascertain if the American carriers were there. The second was a picket line of submarines to detect the movement of the American carriers toward Midway in time for First Mobile Force, First Fleet, and Second Fleet to combine against it. In the event, the first was aborted and the second delayed until after American carriers had sortied.
     </p>
     <p>
      The plan was a compromise and hastily prepared (apparently so it could be launched in time for the anniversary of
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tsushima" title="Battle of Tsushima">
       Tsushima
      </a>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but appeared well thought out, well organized, and finely timed when viewed from a Japanese viewpoint. Against four carriers, two light carriers, 11 battleships, 16 cruisers and 46 destroyers likely to be in the area of the main battle the Americans could field only three carriers, eight cruisers, and 15 destroyers. The disparity appeared crushing. Only in numbers of carrier decks, available aircraft, and submarines was there near parity between the two sides. Despite various frictions developed in the execution, it appearedbarring something extraordinaryYamamoto held all the cards.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Americans were able to learn of the Japanese plans thanks to code breaking of Japanese naval code D (known to the U.S. as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JN-25" title="JN-25">
       JN-25
      </a>
      ). As a result, Admiral
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chester_Nimitz" title="Chester Nimitz">
       Chester Nimitz
      </a>
      , the Pacific Fleet commander, was able to circumvent both of Yamamoto's security measures and position his outnumbered forces in the exact position to conduct a devastating ambush. By Nimitz's calculation, his three available carrier decks, plus Midway, gave him rough parity with Nagumo's First Mobile Force.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following a nuisance raid by Japanese
      <a href="/wiki/Flying_boat" title="Flying boat">
       flying boats
      </a>
      in May,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nimitz dispatched a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minesweeper_(ship)" title="Minesweeper (ship)">
       minesweeper
      </a>
      to guard the intended refueling point for Operation K near
      <a href="/wiki/French_Frigate_Shoals" title="French Frigate Shoals">
       French Frigate Shoals
      </a>
      , causing the reconnaissance mission to be aborted and leaving Yamamoto ignorant of whether Pacific Fleet carriers were still at Pearl Harbor. (It remains unclear why Yamamoto permitted the earlier attack, and why his submarines did not sortie sooner, as reconnaissance was essential to the success of MI.) He also dispatched his carriers toward Midway early, and they passed the intended picket line force of submarines
      <i>
       en route
      </i>
      to their station, negating Yamamoto's back-up security measure. Nimitz's carriers positioned themselves to ambush the
      <i>
       Kido Butai
      </i>
      (Striking Force) when it struck Midway. A token cruiser and destroyer force was sent toward the Aleutians, but otherwise Nimitz ignored them. On June 4, 1942, days before Yamamoto expected them to interfere in the Midway operation, American carrier-based aircraft destroyed the four carriers of the
      <i>
       Kido Butai
      </i>
      , catching the Japanese carriers at an especially vulnerable moment.
     </p>
     <p>
      With his air power destroyed and his forces not yet concentrated for a fleet battle, Yamamoto attempted to maneuver his remaining forces, still strong on paper, to trap the American forces. He was unable to do so because his initial dispositions had placed his surface combatants too far from Midway,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willmott.2C_H.P._1983_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Willmott.2C_H.P._1983-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and because Admiral
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_Spruance" title="Raymond Spruance">
       Raymond Spruance
      </a>
      prudently withdrew to the east in a position to further defend Midway Island, believing (based on a mistaken
      <a href="/wiki/USS_Tambor_(SS-198)#Second_patrol" title="USS Tambor (SS-198)">
       submarine report
      </a>
      ) the Japanese still intended to invade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blair_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-blair-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Not knowing several battleships, including the powerful
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_battleship_Yamato" title="Japanese battleship Yamato">
       <i>
        Yamato
       </i>
      </a>
      , were on the Japanese
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_battle" title="Order of battle">
       order of battle
      </a>
      , he did not comprehend the severe risk of a night surface battle, in which his carriers and cruisers would be at a disadvantage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blair_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-blair-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, his move to the east did avoid the possibility of such a battle taking place. Correctly perceiving he had lost and could not bring surface forces into action, Yamamoto aborted the invasion of Midway and withdrew. The defeat marked the high tide of Japanese expansion.
     </p>
     <p>
      Yamamoto's plan for MI has been the subject of much criticism. Many commentators state it violated the principle of concentration of force, and was overly complex. Others point out similarly complex Allied operations (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_MB8" title="Operation MB8">
       Operation MB8
      </a>
      ) that were successful, and note the extent to which the American intelligence
      <i>
       coup
      </i>
      derailed the operation before it began. Had Yamamoto's dispositions not denied Nagumo adequate pre-attack reconnaissance assets, both the American cryptanalytic success and the unexpected appearance of Fletcher's carriers would have been irrelevant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willmott.2C_H.P._1983_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Willmott.2C_H.P._1983-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Actions_after_Midway">
       Actions after Midway
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Actions after Midway">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="204" data-file-width="262" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          does not
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
           cite
          </a>
          any
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           references or sources
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help improve this section by
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">
           adding citations to reliable sources
          </a>
          . Unsourced material may be challenged and
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           removed
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (February 2013)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The Battle of Midway solidly checked Japanese momentum, but the IJN was still a powerful force and capable of regaining the initiative. They planned to resume the thrust with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_FS" title="Operation FS">
        Operation FS
       </a>
      </i>
      aimed at eventually taking
      <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">
       Samoa
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">
       Fiji
      </a>
      to cut the American life-line to Australia. This was expected to short-circuit the threat posed by General
      <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">
       Douglas MacArthur
      </a>
      and his American and Australian forces in New Guinea. To this end, development of the
      <a href="/wiki/Henderson_Field_(Guadalcanal)" title="Henderson Field (Guadalcanal)">
       airfield on Guadalcanal
      </a>
      continued and attracted the baleful eye of Yamamoto's opposite number, Admiral
      <a href="/wiki/Ernest_King" title="Ernest King">
       Ernest King
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      To prevent the Japanese from regaining the initiative, King ramrodded the idea of an immediate American counterattack through the Joint Chiefs of Staff. This precipitated the
      <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal_Campaign" title="Guadalcanal Campaign">
       American invasion of Guadalcanal
      </a>
      and beat the Japanese to the punch, with Marines landing on the island in August 1942 and starting a bitter struggle that lasted until February 1943 and commenced a battle of attrition Japan could ill afford.
     </p>
     <p>
      Yamamoto remained in command as Commander-in-Chief, retained at least partly to avoid diminishing the morale of the Combined Fleet. However, he had
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_face" title="Lost face">
       lost face
      </a>
      as a result of the Midway defeat and the Naval General Staff were disinclined to indulge in further gambles. This reduced Yamamoto to pursuing the classic defensive Decisive Battle strategy he had attempted to overturn.
     </p>
     <p>
      The naval and land battles at Guadalcanal caught the Japanese over-extended and attempting to support fighting in New Guinea while guarding the Central Pacific and preparing to conduct Operation FS. The FS operation was abandoned and the Japanese attempted to fight in both New Guinea and Guadalcanal at the same time. Already stretched thin, they suffered repeated setbacks due to a lack of shipping, a lack of troops, and a disastrous inability to coordinate Army and Navy activities.
     </p>
     <p>
      Yamamoto committed Combined Fleet units to a series of small attrition actions across the south and central Pacific that stung the Americans, but suffered losses he could ill afford in return. Three major efforts to carry the island precipitated a pair of carrier battles that Yamamoto commanded personally at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Eastern_Solomons" title="Battle of the Eastern Solomons">
       Eastern Solomons
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Santa_Cruz_Islands" title="Battle of the Santa Cruz Islands">
       Santa Cruz Islands
      </a>
      in September and October, and finally a
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_Battle_of_Guadalcanal" title="Naval Battle of Guadalcanal">
       wild pair of surface engagements
      </a>
      in November, all timed to coincide with Japanese Army pushes. The timing of each major battle was successively derailed when the army could not hold up its end of the operation. Yamamoto's naval forces won a few victories and inflicted considerable losses and damage to the U.S. Fleet in several naval battles around Guadalcanal which included the battles of
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Savo_Island" title="Battle of Savo Island">
       Savo Island
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cape_Esperance" title="Battle of Cape Esperance">
       Cape Esperance
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tassafaronga" title="Battle of Tassafaronga">
       Tassafaronga
      </a>
      , but he could never draw the Americans into a decisive fleet action. As a result, the Japanese Navy's strength began to bleed off.
     </p>
     <p>
      There were severe losses of carrier dive-bomber and torpedo-bomber crews in the carrier battles, emasculating the already depleted carrier air groups. Japan could not hope to match the United States in quantities of well-trained replacement pilots, and the quality of both Japanese land-based and naval aviation began declining. Particularly harmful, however, were losses of numerous
      <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">
       destroyers
      </a>
      in the unsuccessful
      <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Express" title="Tokyo Express">
       Tokyo Express
      </a>
      supply runs. The IJN already faced a shortage of such ships, and these losses further exacerbated Japan's already weakened
      <a href="/wiki/Convoy" title="Convoy">
       commerce defense
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parillo_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-parillo-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With Guadalcanal lost in February 1943, there was no further attempt by the Japanese navy to seek a major battle in the Solomon Islands against the U.S. fleet, although smaller attrition battles continued. Yamamoto shifted the load of the air battle away from the depleted carriers groups and to the land-based naval air forces.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death">
       Death
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Death">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Vengeance" title="Operation Vengeance">
       Operation Vengeance
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      To boost morale following the defeat at Guadalcanal, Yamamoto decided to make an inspection tour throughout the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean_Theater_of_World_War_II" title="Pacific Ocean Theater of World War II">
       South Pacific
      </a>
      . On April 14, 1943, the US naval intelligence effort, code-named "
      <a href="/wiki/Magic_(cryptography)" title="Magic (cryptography)">
       Magic
      </a>
      ", intercepted and decrypted a message containing specific details regarding Yamamoto's tour, including arrival and departure times and locations, as well as the number and types of aircraft that would transport and accompany him on the journey. Yamamoto, the itinerary revealed, would be flying from
      <a href="/wiki/Rabaul" title="Rabaul">
       Rabaul
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Balalae_Airport" title="Balalae Airport">
       Balalae Airfield
      </a>
      , on an island near
      <a href="/wiki/Bougainville_Island" title="Bougainville Island">
       Bougainville
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">
       Solomon Islands
      </a>
      , on the morning of April 18, 1943.
     </p>
     <p>
      U.S. President
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">
       Franklin D. Roosevelt
      </a>
      ordered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Secretary of the Navy">
       Secretary of the Navy
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Knox" title="Frank Knox">
       Frank Knox
      </a>
      to "Get Yamamoto." Knox instructed
      <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Operations" title="Chief of Naval Operations">
       Chief of Naval Operations
      </a>
      Admiral
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernest_J._King" title="Ernest J. King">
       Ernest J. King
      </a>
      of Roosevelt's wishes. Admiral King telephoned Admiral
      <a href="/wiki/Chester_W._Nimitz" title="Chester W. Nimitz">
       Chester W. Nimitz
      </a>
      at Pearl Harbor. This mission would be Top Secret and Urgent. Admiral Nimitz consulted Admiral
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_F._Halsey,_Jr." title="William F. Halsey, Jr.">
       William F. Halsey, Jr.
      </a>
      , Commander, South Pacific, then authorized a mission on April 17 to intercept Yamamoto's flight
      <i>
       en route
      </i>
      and shoot it down. A squadron of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USAAF" title="USAAF">
       USAAF
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_P-38_Lightning" title="Lockheed P-38 Lightning">
       Lockheed P-38 Lightning
      </a>
      aircraft were assigned the task as only they possessed the range to intercept and engage. Select pilots from three units were informed that they were intercepting an "important high officer" with no specific name given.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="332" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg/220px-Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg/330px-Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yamamoto_last_image_alive.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Admiral Yamamoto, a few hours before his death, saluting Japanese naval pilots at Rabaul, April 18, 1943
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On the morning of April 18, despite urgings by local commanders to cancel the trip for fear of ambush, Yamamoto's two
      <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_G4M" title="Mitsubishi G4M">
       Mitsubishi G4M
      </a>
      bombers, used as fast transport aircraft without bombs, left Rabaul as scheduled for the 315mi (507km) trip. Sixteen Lightnings intercepted the flight over Bougainville and a dogfight ensued between them and the six escorting
      <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_A6M_Zero" title="Mitsubishi A6M Zero">
       Mitsubishi A6M Zeroes
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Lieutenant" title="First Lieutenant">
       First Lieutenant
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Rex_T._Barber" title="Rex T. Barber">
       Rex T. Barber
      </a>
      engaged the first of the two Japanese transports which turned out to be
      <i>
       T1-323
      </i>
      (Yamamoto's aircraft). He targeted the aircraft with gunfire until it began to spew smoke from its left engine. Barber turned away to attack the other transport as Yamamoto's plane crashed into the jungle.
     </p>
     <p>
      The crash site and body of Yamamoto were found the next day in the jungle north of
      <a href="/wiki/Buin,_Papua_New_Guinea" title="Buin, Papua New Guinea">
       Buin, Papua New Guinea
      </a>
      , by a Japanese search and rescue party, led by army engineer, Lieutenant Hamasuna. According to Hamasuna, Yamamoto had been thrown clear of the plane's wreckage, his white-gloved hand grasping the hilt of his
      <a href="/wiki/Katana" title="Katana">
       katana
      </a>
      , still upright in his seat under a tree. Hamasuna said Yamamoto was instantly recognizable, head dipped down as if deep in thought. A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-mortem" title="Post-mortem">
       post-mortem
      </a>
      of the body disclosed that Yamamoto had received two 0.50-caliber bullet wounds, one to the back of his left shoulder and another to his left lower jaw that exited above his right eye. The Japanese navy doctor examining the body determined that the head wound killed Yamamoto. The more violent details of Yamamoto's death were hidden from the Japanese public; the medical report was whitewashed, changed "on orders from above", according to biographer Hiroyuki Agawa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      His staff cremated his remains at Buin, and the ashes were returned to Tokyo aboard the battleship
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_battleship_Musashi" title="Japanese battleship Musashi">
       <i>
        Musashi
       </i>
      </a>
      , Yamamoto's last flagship. Yamamoto was given a full
      <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">
       state funeral
      </a>
      on June 5, 1943,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      where he received, posthumously, the title of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshal_(Imperial_Japanese_Navy)" title="Marshal (Imperial Japanese Navy)">
       Marshal
      </a>
      and was awarded the
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Chrysanthemum" title="Order of the Chrysanthemum">
       Order of the Chrysanthemum
      </a>
      (1st Class). He was also awarded
      <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
       Nazi Germany
      </a>
      's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knight%27s_Cross_of_the_Iron_Cross_with_Oak_Leaves_and_Swords" title="Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves and Swords">
       Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves and Swords
      </a>
      . Part of his ashes were buried in the public
      <a href="/wiki/Tama_Cemetery" title="Tama Cemetery">
       Tama Cemetery
      </a>
      , Tokyo (), and the remainder at his ancestral burial grounds at the temple of Chuko-ji in
      <a href="/wiki/Nagaoka,_Niigata" title="Nagaoka, Niigata">
       Nagaoka City
      </a>
      . He was succeeded as commander-in-chief of the Combined Fleet by Admiral
      <a href="/wiki/Mineichi_Koga" title="Mineichi Koga">
       Mineichi Koga
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The wreck of the aircraft that carried Yamamoto remains as a tourist attraction in the Bougainville jungle near Moila Point, a few kilometers off the Panguna-Buin road. Signposts can be found near the village of Aku, 24km outside Buin. A path to the wreck has been cut through the jungle, an hour's walk from the road. Other artifacts from the crash site, including the outer wing panel and the Admiral's seat, are at the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Isoroku_Yamamoto_Memorial_Hall_and_Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Isoroku Yamamoto Memorial Hall and Museum (page does not exist)">
       Isoroku Yamamoto Memorial Hall and Museum
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Nagaoka,_Niigata" title="Nagaoka, Niigata">
       Nagaoka, Niigata
      </a>
      , Japan.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">
       Personal life
      </span>
